Australia* Association teetirrtd at ■ very opportune linn for dioiiing en official opinion on the oontrp- verty raised by the pubiiation of Mf. Qoldwin Smith's theories of colonial emsjieipstloji. The Duke of Newcutle din little mora than expteu the opinio", which are entertained by the great majotiiy of iliinkinft nn ; but bii position and experience u Ooloniil Minuter enabled bits to stamp Kbit may bo called the erthodox viewe of the relations of England and her eoirjhki villi *n authority which will bo [ f rvif f ■Mr in corn be tine; th* ingenious arguments of Mr., Ui'U win Smith than ill iho si 11 y nnd cor. • rptusut banter of the Tihicj. Tbo effects of revolution which hu taken place within the Jut twenty yeeri in tbo colonial policy of this country may no summed up in three radical chic at 1. Dome 1 tic independence hu taken the piece of absolute subservience to the will of Iho mother- country. Hearty loyalty hu succeeded to almost universal disaffection. Force has teen superseded by a healthy influence honourably exerted on tbe one side ud fruikly accepted on the other, It in s lunge that tkew common premises should hive lod the Duke of Newcastle ind Mr. Goldwin Smith to precisely opposite com lusions. According to the Professor, cbeje results ere the sure in- dicitisne of so culy srpsration. Awordinfi; to the Minister, thty uo the best possible ffuaian- teea of a luting union for the mutual benefit of bath parlies lo ths subsisting compact- Nine person* out of ten would probably ao-ir* in- stinctively at the conclusion that the Minister ia tight and the Ptofesior wrong, and their judgment may not be (ha liu sound even ■hough ihey might find ii difficult to point out the precise logics! fallacy of the separation 1 henry. In hia conlioversy with the J'mu-i, Mr. Goid- win Smith was able to tome off with an appa- rent victory by quoting the admission that "the lime must come when the colonies will no lixrr/r require our aid, and when it will be better for both that they should set up for themselves." It was, no doubt, a fair argument lo ray that, if cui relations with the colonies ete confessedly so provisional u this admission would make them, it cannot be very unreason- able tu inquire whether the epoch which ii thus confidently predicted may not have already come. 1 he we eh peas of an opponent does not ne- ceturihj! make a st rung cause ; and we ire glad to observe that the Duke of Newcastle, gave no counleBsr.ee to the theory which the Tom* pro- pounded in the very article in which it combated the natural inferences from auch. a doctrine. The bane of political argument of late years has been the facility with which speculative predic- tions hare been accepted as unanswerable ccm- elusions of philosophy. A year or two ago, any one who presumed to 'oubt that .radical Parlia- mentary Heform was imminent would have been laughed at even by the Tory authorities ; nnd the only difficulty which hampered the resistance to Mr. Bright ! agitation was the concession which almost every one bad made, that, however un- palatable the fact might be, the time for a ■weeping electoral revolution had already arrived. What had been 10 boldly asserted on the one side, and ao timidly admitted on the other, proved to be a complete fallacy, though the prediction came near to realise itself in consequence of in acceptance by the very men who regarded it with the utmost aversion. It is worth considering whether, in admitting the certainty of ultimate separation between England and her colonies, we are not falling into an error of the same kind, and playing into the hands of those who are anxioua to hasten the dlvorco which they repaid u in- evitable- In one sense, of course, it maybe laid that the connection between a mother- country and her colonies ranat come to an end, but «Bly i* the a&rne. sense in which, judging from eiptrrtence, the dissolution of the mightiiat and most compact empires may be anticipated u the natural consequence nf the continuous flow of events. No institutions ever had the stamp of per- manence more strongly marked upon them than those of our country, end yet few would vendue to say that Macaulny'a New Zsaiander may not witness their decay. Permanence cannot bs absolutely predicted of anything human, and least of all of political Institutions. In this sense, therefore, it may bo true in terms that the time for the mancipation of the colonica must surely come. But if it means no Bore than this, the usertion is the emptiest of platitudes ; ud if it does mean more, we believe that it is both uutrue and extremely misohievaai, inu- much as it places an almost impregnable position at the command of specula- tive politicians. What the Tim,, pro- bubly meant Is ssy by its unguarded admission was, that she connexion between England and her colonise was essentially provisional and temporary— that it could never ripen into any permanent union— and th»t, one by one, the colonies must be thrown off as they arrive m maturity, just u the children of it family eman- cipate themselves when they arrive at ream of diet, \tioo. If thin he accepted u true philosophy, the only answer that can possibly be given to Mr Ooldwin Smith is, that the event which he looks lor ™ the immediate future may perhaps be postponed for another fifty or a hundred yean. But, almost univsrsaJ u the notion of an ultimate severance between England and her colonies hu become, we believe that it depends much more on the loose analogy of the paternal relation than on any sure grounds of experience or rational con- jecture, Experience there in none to guide u, Nothing like the eiisting status of a British colony was ever seen until within a Terr few yem Hhta i i, t™. leaches u, what. have enabled u, to fortsee— that a colony which is coerced and plundered will cut or! n, sllegianc. as soon u it is strong or which the watU h u produce,!/ ample „ore, are wholly beside the ^uution *hich is now raised. fro neither eoerc* „„ plunder our colonies, and, so far u a brief si perument can prove anything, the remit, of our modern policy encourage the idea that time end, rather to strengthen than to break such ties u those which now bind the British Empire ZE^lX 1 *™* iT" tlBg 'Po- tation wrdoh we deprecate, we may ear that the continuous lncr eu . of nu iJ lfr "* Hm .and confidence is sot. symptom of mm* mm* ■, m we force of arm, » mature colon j which desires ii t Sff" U * lt ° ge ' h " visiooar, w, ... It ia tcmceuvabl* that a young nation growing rapidly into power might be galled even by the merely nominal sovereignty of a dist merely nominal sovereignty and that, at a certain stare of polities.! develop nttnt,it would so longer be content to abandon all control over questions of foreign policy. But, assuming that such sentiments must ultimately prevail, there ia nothing to make an amicable divorce the neceasary and only consequence. If philoaophical politicians would be content to leave future history towork out her own problems, without lnatsting that events must follow In tbe track of their preconceived theories, they' would be safer guides than they are now. There are many waya besides separation ia which the institutions of our colonial empire might accommodate themselves to the rapid increase of wealth, population, and power which promises to attend upon our various colonies. Whether any of them will ever attain a relative importance sufficient to deprive England of her claim to a metropolitan posi- tion may well be doubted; but even In so extreme and remote a cue aa this, it might be quite u easy to readjust the old bond on terms of greater equality al to snap it altogether. Even the possibility of the friendly separation which is assumed to be the euthanasia of our colonial dominion i, by no means clear ; for the desire to separate, which must precede the divorce, could acarcely grow prednminant without something analogous to the irritation which an actual conflict would provoke. Certainly it is a bold assumption to take for granted the absolute certainty of a transaction the like of which hu never been atttmpted since the world began. It would perhaps be t«ss extravagant to imagine a con- tinual approach on the part of England and her colonies to the realisation of some idea of a Federal Empire, which the democratic machinery of the 1 tailed Ptales has so signs!] y failed to construrt. Hut it is a 1 idle to trice one course as another for the distant fnture. AH that we are juitifled in saying is, that the assumption that the seeds of dissolution are sown in our colonial empire is wholly improved, and that, while tbe tendency of colonial feeling continues to be more and more in favour of union, it is neither philosophical nor politic to assume the necessity of s future ssvsrance. The form of tbe con- nexton may change again, aa it hu charged already, to suit the altered circum- stance* of the colonist end the higher tone of political morality at borne : but the connexion itself is not likely to cease so long u a common interest and mutual sentiment* of affection exist to hind England and her colonies together.
